0312430000|5.0|Let me make this clear up front -- I would read just about anything Atul Gawande writes. He's simply a terrific writer and storyteller.<br /><br />As you can tell from the title, this book is about checklists (not simple \\"to do\\" lists). He talks about how in the medical field, the amount of knowledge we have gained far surpasses the ability for one individual (such as a surgeon), or even a team of people, to fully comprehend.<br /><br />He begins with the history of checklists in aviation, and then talks about checklist systems used in building. I think we take for granted how safe, reliable and efficient the processes of flying airplanes and constructing buildings has become. It's apparent that checklists are front and center in their success.<br /><br />This book is useful for anyone who works in a field that is complex. It has excellent examples of checklists in the financial services (investment) field, as well as the aforementioned aviation, building and medicine fields. He introduced a checklist via WHO funding and saw dramatic improvements in the surgical field. In short, it's a no-brainer to introduce checklists in a variety of different endeavors. If you don't believe me, read the book! After all, it's an easy, short read and only took me a few hours to finish.<br /><br />I was interested in this book because of my own experiences with checklists. When I worked with a group of contracts professionals, I pushed my boss to establish a standard checklist for the contracts folks. He agreed and we went ahead and put together a fairly comprehensive but easy to use checklist, with the help of various other disciplines. Unfortunately, my boss was unceremoniously fired just as we were to present the checklist to management for approval and insertion into standard company processes. And much to my chagrin, the checklist basically died in utero as successive people didn't deem it important. Another funny aspect of this process was how most of the contracts people -- most of whom were in desperate need of assistance with their work, as it was sloppy, uninspired and far from standardized -- reacted. Just like the surgeons in Gawande's book. They viewed it not as a tool to help them manage the highly complex job they have and reduce errors, but as an affront to their skill set, experience and ego. This reaction shocked me, as I always felt that I'd love to have a checklist in every aspect of my job. Unfortunately, my area of the legal field (IP) isn't necessarily checklist friendly all the time, although this book has inspired me to make the attempt.<br /><br />I highly recommend this book, along with most of the other books Gawande has written.<br /><br />9/10

B009T3AHDE|5.0|A good David Cronenberg film is never something easy to watch, it's never something shallow or even likeable.  Cronenberg's filming style has always been about strangeness and perplexity, the portrayal of his characters' own strangeness and perplexity.  There's not much humor in them, they speak in epigrams, often dark ones, and come in and out of the film in a very enigmatic way.    However, the progression of the film is totally cerebral and quite complex.  It's claustrophobic too although this does not come from the closed spaces in which the story unwraps but from a deeper and more obscure anguish, one that can not be traced and is consequently expressed in a labyrinthine and disconcerting way, same as de Lillo's book and faithful to Cronenberg's own filming style.    Cosmopolis gives the Canadian director the chance to connect his more recent themes such as corporate politics, how the past shapes the present and the origin of violence with his older and more strange themes like the mechanization of the conscience, the correlation between technology and the body and the link between biology and power.  All of these topics and many more are present in the film, and these are exactly the themes that have placed David Cronenberg as one of the most original directors among contemporary filmmakers.    Pattison delivers a fine performance far removed from the ones he's done in more commercial and often stupid films.  Let's hope it does not take so long before he's able to shake off the s***ty Twilight stigma.    Cosmopolis is a towering achievement, another one in Cronenberg's gigantic and marvelous filmography, a real treasure... but only for those who think.
